    \section{Dropout statistics data
analyis}\label{dropout-statistics-data-analyis}

This notebook summarizes the efforts made in the analysis of statistical
dropout data for the standard 4130cu. in. array with Bolt LLXT guns.
Other arrays or dropout rules may easily be analyzed by saving this
Jupyter Notebook with a new name and feed in the raw statistics (dan)
files for the relavant source. Dropout rules are specified in this
notebook under the Heading called Dropout Rule.

    \subsection{Motivation}\label{motivation}

Dropout specs and modeling has been a topic for discussion for years:
The tolerance specs are sharp, resulting in a red or green box in a
dropout matrix, but the matrix is based on a set of assumptions that are
quite inaccurate.

Source modeling code and calibration: old vs new matters

Temperature sensitivity

We are assuming a static and 100\% correctly deployed source.

Dropouts are done on vertical farfields, even if relevant farfields are
mostly non-vertical

Origin of specs (operational more than geophysical, ``nice round
numbers'').

Etc.

Reshoots due to dropouts are costly. A revision is in line with a
streamlining/cost-cutting philosophy There is no guarantee that a
reshoot results in improved data quality Current practice of bespoke
dropout matrix per project is error prone and resource demanding. And
triggers discussion if new modeling should be used to allow for more
dropped guns.

Shot-to-shot designature: Some possibility to correct for a range of bad
shots.

    Include perl script

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
{\color{incolor}In [{\color{incolor}1}]:} \PY{n}{parentdir} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+s+s2}{\PYZdq{}}\PY{l+s+s2}{release\PYZus{}280}\PY{l+s+s2}{\PYZdq{}} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{}Convention: Nucleus version. Used for dan file input reading}
        \PY{n}{subdir} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+s+s2}{\PYZdq{}}\PY{l+s+s2}{batch\PYZus{}3265}\PY{l+s+s2}{\PYZdq{}} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{}Convention: Source configuration. Used for dan file input reading.}
        \PY{n}{sourceconf} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+s+s2}{\PYZdq{}}\PY{l+s+s2}{3265}\PY{l+s+s2}{\PYZdq{}} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{}Used for defining input dan file names and number of lines to skip. And gun number mapping}
        \PY{n}{guntype} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+s+s2}{\PYZdq{}}\PY{l+s+s2}{H}\PY{l+s+s2}{\PYZdq{}} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{}Used to define input dan file name}
        \PY{n}{mastermatrix} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+s+s2}{\PYZdq{}}\PY{l+s+s2}{3265}\PY{l+s+s2}{\PYZdq{}} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{}Used to select mastermatrix. Also which version of the 4130 }
        \PY{n}{dropout\PYZus{}rule} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+s+s2}{\PYZdq{}}\PY{l+s+s2}{strictness\PYZus{}3}\PY{l+s+s2}{\PYZdq{}} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{}Used to sub select master matrix to vary the strictness }

    \subsection{Setting some parameters for reading the
input}\label{setting-some-parameters-for-reading-the-input}

    Below, the header of two dan-files (broadband and
conventional/cross-correlation) are listed to confirm the modeling
parameters used in Nucleus. First specify the path to the dan files for
the dataset to analyze:

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]

    \subsection{Modeling Parameters}\label{modeling-parameters}

This report analyse statistical values of the following dropout
scenarios:

Temperatures: 5, 10, 15, 20, 25

Depths 4-9m; 1m increment

Subarray separation: 8 and 10m

Bolt and GII guns

Broadband (refl coeff zero) and conventional (refl coeff -1)

Total: 123 360 combinations

60 source/temperature combinations

2 modelings per source (broadband and conv)

2 gun types

All single and two gun dropouts with spare gun substitution

514 dropout combinations per source (nominal source always used as
reference)

The datasets are analyzed separately for Gun type to detect systematic
differences.

    Defining the column names:

    The follwing is a lambda function that converts to string and adds zeros
to a total length of 2 characters to the input x (which could be an
int). Is later used to convert all gun numbers from int to string. (3 to
"03", for example)

    Adding all the string representations for gun numbering in a dedicated
set of columns. Is later used to make array\_gun\_number index with a
logical order.

    Next step is to copy dataframes to identical copy with "all" postfix.

dataframes. The "all" dataframes will contain the unfiltered data
material.

    print("Number of combinations for all(raw) combinations (one gun, two
gun and spare)") print(len(stat\_one\_gun\_bb\_raw),
len(stat\_two\_gun\_bb\_raw), len(stat\_spare\_gun\_bb\_raw))

len(stat\_spare\_gun\_cc\_raw)) print("Number of combinations for legal
combinations (one gun, two gun and spare)")
print(len(stat\_one\_gun\_bb\_legal), len(stat\_two\_gun\_bb\_legal),
len(stat\_spare\_gun\_bb\_legal)) print(len(stat\_one\_gun\_cc\_legal),
len(stat\_two\_gun\_cc\_legal), len(stat\_spare\_gun\_cc\_legal))

    \subsection{Dropout Rule}\label{dropout-rule}

Master Matrix suggestion:

Illegal dropouts are defined by operationally suitable gun combinations
and not by ``geophysical'' specs:

All substitutions allowed

Same rule applied regardless of depth, gun type, subarray separation,
temperature.

We probably have to live with some outliers (keep shooting in some of
the red boxes). This notebook investigates the impact on a statistical
level.

The goal for the new dropout rule is: ``No single gun dropout is a show-
stopper. All failing big guns in clusters may be substituted''

    \subsubsection{Traditional matrix}\label{traditional-matrix}

    Will add columns bb\_Legal and cc\_Legal

may be substituted {[}1, 13{]}, {[}1, 14{]}, {[}2, 1{]}, \# 100c may be

may be substituted {[}3, 11{]}, {[}3, 12{]}, {[}3, 13{]}, {[}3, 14{]}{]}

(stat\_one\_gun\_bb\_legal.dropgun1 ==

\section{Below it is assumed that any two gun combination with illegal
one gun is also
illegal.}\label{below-it-is-assumed-that-any-two-gun-combination-with-illegal-one-gun-is-also-illegal.}

for i in range(len(illegal\_one\_gun)): stat\_two\_gun\_bb\_legal =
stat\_two\_gun\_bb\_legal.drop(stat\_two\_gun\_bb\_legal{[}(stat\_two\_gun\_bb\_legal.droparray1
== illegal\_one\_gun{[}i{]}{[}0{]}) \&
(stat\_two\_gun\_bb\_legal.dropgun1 == illegal\_one\_gun{[}i{]}{[}1{]})
\textbar{} (stat\_two\_gun\_bb\_legal.droparray2 ==
illegal\_one\_gun{[}i{]}{[}0{]}) \& (stat\_two\_gun\_bb\_legal.dropgun2
== illegal\_one\_gun{[}i{]}{[}1{]}){]}.index, axis=0)
stat\_two\_gun\_cc\_legal =
stat\_two\_gun\_cc\_legal.drop(stat\_two\_gun\_cc\_legal{[}(stat\_two\_gun\_cc\_legal.droparray1
== illegal\_one\_gun{[}i{]}{[}0{]}) \&
(stat\_two\_gun\_cc\_legal.dropgun1 == illegal\_one\_gun{[}i{]}{[}1{]})
\textbar{} (stat\_two\_gun\_cc\_legal.droparray2 ==
illegal\_one\_gun{[}i{]}{[}0{]}) \& (stat\_two\_gun\_cc\_legal.dropgun2
== illegal\_one\_gun{[}i{]}{[}1{]}){]}.index, axis=0)

    First plot is of all two gun dropouts

    \section{Stop}\label{stop}

    \paragraph{Above plot comments}\label{above-plot-comments}

Typical tolerance specs are marked with vertical line for reference
only. Note that orange dataset (master matrix ``passed'')is a
sub-selection of blue dataset (partly transparent makes the orange
slightly brown) We observe that the master matrix drop out rule gets rid
of most ``bad'' combinations, according to the current specs, but also
some good ones that would be within the current specs. The number of
legal combinations that are above current specs are few and quite
marginal.

    Second plot is for single gun dropouts:

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]

\end{Verbatim}


    \paragraph{Above plot comments}\label{above-plot-comments}

Single gun dropouts are a lot more likely than two gun dropouts. The
Master Matrix rule is rejecting practically all bad combinations,
according to traditional specs Note: Spares not enabled

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
{\color{incolor}In [{\color{incolor} }]:} \PY{n}{figplot}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{3}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{stat\PYZus{}spare\PYZus{}gun\PYZus{}bb\PYZus{}raw}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{stat\PYZus{}spare\PYZus{}gun\PYZus{}bb\PYZus{}legal}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{stat\PYZus{}spare\PYZus{}gun\PYZus{}cc\PYZus{}raw}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{stat\PYZus{}spare\PYZus{}gun\PYZus{}cc\PYZus{}legal}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{Spare Gun dropout statistics 4135H 2.8.0 k=2.0}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{4135H\PYZus{}spare\PYZus{}280.png}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}
\end{Verbatim}


    \paragraph{Above plot comments}\label{above-plot-comments}

All spare substitutions allowed, so light blue bars are completely
obscures by darker blue bars. We observe some substitutions that for
some parameter combinations could be problematic.

    For more in depth analysis and fancier plots (using Pyhton Seaborn
module), we join the datasets. First, we'll strip off some columns
before the join and add columns for Scenario (which can have the
following values: "One gun dropoout", "Two gun dropout" and "Spare
substitution") and for Status (which can have the following values:
"All" or "Legal"). At the end, all the data are concatenated to a
dataset (panda dataframe) called comb\_data\_bb (bb reflecting that this
is the broadband dataset)

    Then we repeat the process for the cc data (cross-correlation)

    \subsection{Box Plot}\label{box-plot}

The box plot (also known as a box-and-whisker plot) can tell us about
how our dataset is distributed. It shows us the range of our dataset,
gives us an idea about where a significant portion of our data lies, and
whether or not any outliers are present.

How to interpret a box plot:

-The box represents the interquartile range (The difference between the
upper and lower quartiles (Q3--Q1) of a data set. This range is used as
a measure of data spread: spanning 50\% of a data set and eliminating
the influence of outliers (the highest and lowest quarters of a data set
are removed).)

-The line in the middle of the box is the median

-The end lines are the first and third quartiles (In order to determine
the interquartile range, a data set is divided into four equal parts.
Each separating value is called a quartile (the first, the second,
etc.). The second quartile is the median)

-The diamonds show outliers

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
{\color{incolor}In [{\color{incolor} }]:} \PY{n}{sns}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{set\PYZus{}style}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s2}{\PYZdq{}}\PY{l+s+s2}{darkgrid}\PY{l+s+s2}{\PYZdq{}}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{n}{sns}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{set\PYZus{}palette}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s2}{\PYZdq{}}\PY{l+s+s2}{pastel}\PY{l+s+s2}{\PYZdq{}}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{n}{sns}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{set\PYZus{}context}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s2}{\PYZdq{}}\PY{l+s+s2}{paper}\PY{l+s+s2}{\PYZdq{}}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{n}{sns}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{set\PYZus{}palette}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s2}{\PYZdq{}}\PY{l+s+s2}{Paired}\PY{l+s+s2}{\PYZdq{}}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{}plt.figure(figsize=(12,8))}
        \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{}plt.axhline(20, color=\PYZsq{}red\PYZsq{})}
        \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{}sns.boxplot(data=comb\PYZus{}data, x=\PYZsq{}Scenario\PYZsq{}, y=\PYZsq{}MaxPhase\PYZsq{}, hue=\PYZsq{}Status\PYZsq{});}
\end{Verbatim}


    For reference the 20 degree maxphase limit is plotted as a red line.
Observations:

One gun dropout (which is far more likely than two gun dropouts): All
legal (master matrix filtered) or 100\% of combinations are within the
20 phase spec

Two gun dropouts: More than 75\% of legal dropouts are within spec

Spare gun dropouts: More than 75\% of legal dropouts are within spec

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
{\color{incolor}In [{\color{incolor} }]:} \PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{figure}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{figsize}\PY{o}{=}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{12}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mi}{8}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{axhline}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{3}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{color}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{red}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{n}{sns}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{boxplot}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{data}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{comb\PYZus{}data}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{x}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{Scenario}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{y}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{MaxdB}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{hue}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{Status}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{;}
\end{Verbatim}


    \subsection{Violin Plots}\label{violin-plots}

Violin plots provide more information than box plots because instead of
mapping each individual data point, we get an estimation of the dataset
from the KDE (Kernel Density Estimation).

There are two KDE plots that are symmetrical along the center line. A
white dot represents the median. The thick black line in the center of
each violin represents the interquartile range. The lines that extend
from the center are the confidence intervals - just on the box plots, a
violin plot also displays the 95\% confidence interval.

    \subsubsection{Checking correlation between some statistical
values}\label{checking-correlation-between-some-statistical-values}

To answer questions like: If x-corr is bad, is always MaxPhase equally
bad? Does traditional broadband specs capture bad combinations also
according to other specs?

\end{Verbatim}


    Comment to above figure: The red spec lines form a 4-field matrix.

Many combinations are bad using both specs (lower right field).

But many combinations that fullfill the x-corr spec, fail the MaxPhase
spec (upper right field), also quite significantly (\textgreater{}30
degrees).

Also many combinations that meet the MaxPhase spec fails the x-corr spec
(lower left field).

Note that dark dots are still legal combinations according to the
MasterMatrix. Light dots are all combinations.

    \subsection{What about traditional sets of
specs?}\label{what-about-traditional-sets-of-specs}

In previous section, we looked at the dependencies between single specs.
So what about traditional sets of specs?

Broadband MC specs (5-200Hz, refl coeff = 0):

AvgdB: 0.85

MaxdB: 3.0

MaxPhase: 20

Conventional (old MC) specs (10-70Hz, refl coeff = -1)

x-corr: 0.998

AvgdB: 1.2

MaxdB: 3.0

Let us apply this rule as filters to the "All" datasets

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
